Thesis and Mu'tazila Classification of Non-Enduring Accidents
States thesis and Mu'tazila classification of non-enduring accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental thesis that accidents do not subsist for two moments of time, and the Mu'tazila classification of which accidents are non-enduring.

Core Idea
Accidents are divided into those that never last two moments and those that may, according to the Mu'tazila.

This lesson sets the stage for the seventh objective of the First Observatory: the claim that accidents do not persist across two moments. It then presents the Mu'tazila position, which distinguishes colors, tastes, and smells as non-enduring, while knowledges, volitions, sounds, and speech are enduring, with debate about motion and rest.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the clear structure: first the thesis, then the classification, then a pointer to later discussion.
The passage uses a parallel structure: 'الألوان والطعوم والروائح دون العلوم والإرادات...'
2
Identify the thesis
The thesis is stated at the beginning: 'أن العرض لا يبقي زمانين' – the accident does not subsist for two moments. This is the claim to be examined.
This is the core of the objective.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila classification
The Mu'tazila list specific types: colors, tastes, and smells are said to be non-enduring. Knowledges, volitions, sounds, and types of speech are not included in that list (i.e., they may endure). There is a dispute about motion and rest.
This classification is important for later arguments.
4
Reflect on the exceptions
Notice that not all accidents are treated the same. The Mu'tazila make a distinction, and even have internal disagreement. This sets up the need for a deeper argument, which the philosophers provide.
The phrase 'خلاف' indicates the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد السابع : أن العرض لا يبقي زمانين الألوان والطعوم والروائح دون العلوم والإرادات والأصوات وأنواع الكلام للمعتزلة في بقاء الحركة والسكون خلاف كما ستعرفه في مباحث الأكوان (قالوا ) أي الفلاسفة وما لا يبقى من الأعراض السيالة ( يختص إمكانه (بوقته الذي وجد فيه ( لا قبل ولا بعد ) أي لا يمكن أن يوجد قبل ذلك الوقت ولا بعده لاستناده إلى سلسلة مقتضية لذلك الاختصاص
The First Observatory - the Seventh Objective: that the accident does not subsist for two moments. Colors, tastes, and smells – not knowledges, volitions, sounds, and types of speech – for the Mu'tazila there is disagreement about the subsistence of motion and rest, as you will know in the investigations of accidents. (They said), i.e. the philosophers: 'And what does not subsist of the flowing accidents: its possibility is specific to its time in which it exists, not before nor after,' i.e. it cannot exist before that time nor after it, because it is based on a chain necessitating that specificity.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of the seventh objective: that the accident does not subsist for two moments of time. It first notes a disagreement among the Mu'tazila regarding which accidents subsist: they hold that colors, tastes, and smells do not subsist, while knowledges, volitions, sounds, and types of speech might, with further disagreement on motion and rest. Then it presents the view of the philosophers. The philosophers assert that what does not subsist of the 'flowing accidents' (الأعراض السيالة) – accidents that are in constant renewal – has its possibility of existence strictly confined to its own moment. The possibility is not general; it cannot exist before or after that specific time. This specificity is due to a chain of causes (سلسلة) that necessitates it. The argument is that the accident’s existence depends on conditions that are only fully present in that particular moment, so its possibility is tied to that moment alone.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: All accidents are the same regarding subsistence.
Correction: The Mu'tazila explicitly distinguish between types of accidents: some do not subsist, others do, and there is debate about motion and rest.

The Mu'tazila classify accidents into non-enduring (colors, tastes, smells) and enduring (knowledges, volitions, sounds, speech), with a dispute about motion and rest.

Commit to one action today that prioritizes enduring knowledge over fleeting entertainment.

The Philosophers' Argument: Time-Specific Possibility of Flowing Accidents
Presents philosophers' argument for time-specific possibility of flowing accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that the possibility of non-enduring accidents is confined to their specific moment of existence because of a causal chain.

Core Idea
Non-enduring accidents cannot exist at any other time because their possibility is tied to a moment-specific causal chain.

The philosophers now provide a deeper reason for why some accidents do not subsist for two moments. They focus on what they call 'flowing accidents' (الأعراض السيالة) and argue that these accidents have a possibility that is strictly limited to the moment they occur. This limitation is not arbitrary but depends on a chain of causes that only exists at that particular time.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' statement
First, read the Arabic phrase: 'وما لا يبقى من الأعراض السيالة يختص إمكانه بوقته الذي وجد فيه لا قبل ولا بعد'. Notice the key terms: الأعراض السيالة, إمكان, اختصاص.
The phrase is a complete argument in one sentence.
2
Unpack the claim
The claim is that the possibility of a non-enduring accident is specific (يختص) to its time. It cannot exist before or after that time. This is a strong constraint.
Possibility here is 'occurrence possibility' (إمكان وقوعي), not essential possibility.
3
Understand the reason
The reason given is 'لاستناده إلى سلسلة مقتضية لذلك الاختصاص' – it is based on a chain that necessitates that specificity. The causal chain is unique to that moment.
This is a causal argument, not a tautology.
4
Distinguish from essential possibility
Note the common misunderstanding: the philosophers do not deny that the accident has an 'essential possibility' that is timeless. They are speaking about the possibility of actual occurrence, which is conditioned by external causes.
The source notes this nuance in the common_misunderstanding.

Source Text

(They said), i.e. the philosophers: 'And what does not subsist of the flowing accidents: its possibility is specific to its time in which it exists, not before nor after,' i.e. it cannot exist before that time nor after it, because it is based on a chain necessitating that specificity.

Then it presents the view of the philosophers. Misconception: The philosophers deny any possibility for the accident to exist at another time simply because it does not exist there.

Abu Hashim's Proof that Composition Subsists in Two Substances
Abu Hashim argues that composition is an accident subsisting in two substances and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hashim's argument that composition is a real accident requiring two loci, and the objection that a free agent could cause cohesion instead.

Core Idea
Composition is a positive accident that cannot inhere in one substance alone.

Abu Hashim, a prominent Mu'tazilite theologian, offers a proof that composition (al-ta'līf) is an accident that must subsist in two substances. He begins by noting the difficulty of separating the parts of a body, arguing that this difficulty cannot be caused by a mere non-existent but must be due to a real positive attribute. He then shows that this attribute cannot inhere in a single part, so it must be shared by two. An objection is raised and answered, defending the reality of composition as an accident with two loci.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then the literal translation. Notice the flow of the argument: starting from the observation of difficulty in separation, leading to the conclusion that composition is a positive attribute subsisting in two parts.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ولا يقوم التأليف بكل واحد من الجزئين' – this is a key point.
2
Follow the proof step by step
Break the argument into its logical stages: (1) bodies have parts whose disjunction is difficult, (2) this difficulty must be caused by a real attribute (not a non-existent), (3) that attribute is composition, (4) composition cannot inhere in one part alone, so it must inhere in both parts together (not their aggregate).
The phrase 'ولو قال : ولا يقوم بواحد من الجزأين لكان أظهر' suggests a refinement of expression.
3
Analyze the objection and answer
The objection denies that the difficulty is due to composition, attributing it instead to the free agent (God) who directly creates the adhesion. Consider why Abu Hashim would reject this – because it would make composition unnecessary and contradict the intuition that the difficulty is inherent in the body itself.
The answer is simply 'denial' (manʿ); the text does not provide a detailed refutation here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبو هاشم: التأليف عرض وأنه يقوم بجوهرين أكثر أما الأول وهو كونه عرضاً يقوم بجوهرين فلان من الجسم ما يصعب انفكاكه وانفصال أجزائه بعضها عن بعض وليس ذلك العسر في الانفكاك إلا لتأليف يوجب ذلك العسر إذ لولاه لما صَعُبَ الانفكاك بين أجزائه كما في المتجاورات ولا يتصور إيجاب العسر وصعوبة الانفكاك نفي العدم المحض فهو يعني التأليف صفة ثبوتية موجودة موجبة لصعوبة الانفصال ولا يقوم التأليف بكل واحد من الجزئين ضرورة أي لا يجوز أن يقوم بهذا الجزء فقط ولا بذاك الجزء فقط لأنَّ التأليف لا يعقل في أمر واحد بالضرورة ولو قال : ولا يقوم بواحد من الجزأين لكان أظهر فهو قائم بهما أي بكل واحد منهما معاً لا بمجموعها من حيث هو مجموع وإلا كان المحل واحداً وهو المطلوب وجوابه منع أن عسر الانفكاك أجزاء بعض الأجسام للتأليف القائم بتلك الأجزاء بل للفاعل المختار الذي ألصق باختياره بعض تلك الأجزاء ببعض على وجه يصعب الانفكاك به
And Abu Hashim said: Composition (al-ta'līf) is an accident, and it subsists in two substances or more. As for the first part, that it is an accident subsisting in two substances, it is because in the body there is difficulty of its being disjoined and its parts separating from one another. And that difficulty of disjunction is not except due to a composition that necessitates that difficulty, for if not for it, the disjunction between its parts would not be difficult, as in the case of things placed side by side. And it is inconceivable that the causing of difficulty and hardship of disjunction be a mere non-existent, so it—meaning composition—is a positive attribute (ṣifa thubūtiyya) existing, necessitating the difficulty of separation. And composition does not subsist in each one of the two parts necessarily, i.e., it is not permissible that it subsist in this part only nor in that part only, because composition is not intelligible in a single thing necessarily. And if he had said: 'and it does not subsist in one of the two parts', it would be clearer. So it subsists in both of them, i.e., in each one of them together, not in their aggregate as an aggregate, for then the locus would be one. And that is the desired conclusion. Its answer: the denial that the difficulty of disjunction of parts of some bodies is due to the composition subsisting in those parts, but rather due to the free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār) who, by His choice, attached some of those parts to others in a manner that makes disjunction difficult.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a proof by Abu Hashim (the Mu'tazilite theologian) that composition (al-ta'līf) is an accident that subsists in two substances, with an objection and its answer. The argument begins by establishing that bodies have parts that are difficult to separate. This difficulty (ʿusr al-infikāk) cannot be due to a mere non-existent; it must be caused by a real positive attribute (ṣifa thubūtiyya), which is composition. This composition cannot inhere in a single part because composition between two things is not conceptually conceivable in one thing; it requires two loci. Therefore, it must subsist in each part together, not in a single locus that is the aggregate. The objection is that the difficulty of separation might be caused by the free agent (God) who directly creates the adhesion, not by an accident of composition. The answer is a denial of that objection, maintaining that the difficulty is indeed due to the composition itself. The passage thus defends the reality of composition as an accident with two loci.

Key Terms
التأليف (composition)
العرض (accident)
المحل (locus)
الصفة الثبوتية (positive attribute)
عسر الانفكاك (difficulty of disjunction)
الفاعل المختار (free agent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Think that composition is merely a relation or a non-existent mental concept.
Correction: Abu Hashim argues that composition must be a real positive attribute existing in two substances, because it causes real difficulty of separation, which a non-existent cannot cause.

Checkpoint
Why must composition subsist in two loci according to Abu Hashim's proof?
A) Because composition is a relation between two things
B) Because difficulty of separation indicates a positive attribute that cannot inhere in a single part
C) Because the free agent can only attach two things
D) Because the body is composed of two atoms

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim's proof establishes composition as a real accident subsisting in two substances, grounded in the observable difficulty of separating parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a sin or bad habit you find difficult to leave. According to Abu Hashim's reasoning, that difficulty is evidence of a real 'composition' in your soul—a positive attribute that makes separation hard. What is that composition? Is it love of the sin, fear of what people think, or something else? Will you today ask Allah to help you examine that composition and seek to replace it with a firm composition of taqwa?
?
Do you ever attribute your spiritual struggles solely to 'Qadr' without examining the real compositions (habits, attachments, relationships) that make change difficult? How might recognizing the reality of those 'accidents' change your approach to repentance and self-reform? What is one composition you will actively work to dismantle this week?
?
Consider your relationship with the Quran. Is there a 'composition' that subsists between you and the Word of Allah? The difficulty you feel in being consistent with recitation and reflection points to a real bond—either weak or strong. Priority and First Property of Quantity
Sanusi explains why quantity is introduced first and defines its first property: divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why quantity is prioritized over other categories in the study of categories and what its first property (divisibility) entails.

Core Idea
Quantity is prior because it is more general in existence than quality.

This lesson introduces the Second Observatory on Quantity. Al-Sanusi explains why quantity is placed before other categories: because it is more general in existence than quality, and one of its divisions (continuous/discrete) is more firmly existent than relational accidents. He then begins to define the first property of quantity: divisibility. The text, though truncated, sets the foundation for understanding the properties of quantity.

1
Read the passage and note the reason for priority

The passage states that quantity has three properties. The first is that it accepts division. Division is then said to be used in two senses. One of them is 'conceptual division' (القسمة الوهمية). Try to identify this in the Arabic.
The text cuts off before explaining the second meaning.

First Two Properties of Quantity and the Substrate Principle
Al-Jurjani defines the first two properties of quantity and the substrate principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two properties of quantity—how quantity enables divisibility and why continuous quantity cannot accept splitting—due to the substrate principle.

Core Idea
Quantity makes things divisible, but continuous quantity cannot split—only matter does.

Al-Jurjani begins his discussion of quantity by distinguishing its two types and then explains the first two properties: divisibility and non-acceptance of splitting. Understanding why continuous quantity cannot split requires grasping the substrate principle: the substrate must remain with the accepted attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
Pay attention to the enumeration of properties.
2
Identify the two types of quantity and division
Note the distinction between continuous and discrete quantity, and the two types of actual division: separation (fasl) and splitting (fakk).
3
Understand the first property: divisibility through quantity
The first property is that quantity occurs in body and accidents, making them divisible. Without quantity, division is inconceivable.
This shows that divisibility is not inherent but follows from quantity.
4
Understand the second property: no splitting for continuous quantity
Continuous quantity (magnitude) does not accept splitting because the substrate remains with the accepted; if splitting occurred, the original magnitude would cease and new ones arise.
The principle: the substrate must persist for something to be truly receptive.
5
Reflect on the objection and its refutation
The passage ends by addressing an objection about theologians denying quantity. Reflect on why the explanation refutes it.
The refutation relies on the distinction between quantity and matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد الأول : خواص الكم وأقسامه. وقد مرّ أن هذا المعنى شامل للكم المتصل والمنفصل. وعلى القسمة الفعلية وهي الفصل والفك سواء كان بالقطع أو بالكسر. والمعنى الأول من خواص الكم عروضه للجسم ولسائر الأعراض يعني باقيها بواسطة اقتران الكمية بها، فإنك إذا تصورت شيئاً منها، ولم تعتبر معه عدداً ولا مقداراً لم يمكن لك فرض انقسامه. والمعنى الثاني لا يقبله الكم المتصل الذي هو المقدار، فإن القابل يبقى مع المقبول وإلا لم يكن قابلاً له حقيقة بالضرورة. وعند الفك والفصل الوارد على الجسم لا يبقى الكم أي المقدار الأول بعينه؛ لأنه متصل واحد في حد ذاته لا مفصل فيه أصلاً، بل يزول ويحصل هناك كمان أي مقداران آخران لم يكونا موجودين بالفعل وإلا كان في متصل واحد متصلات غير متناهية بحسب الانقسامات الممكنة. نعم الكم المتصل الحال في المادة الجسمية يعد المادة لقبول القسمة الانفكاكية وإن لم يمكن اجتماع ذلك الكم مع تلك القسمة. عليه البيان فلا يرد ما قيل: إنه لا نزاع في وجود الأين والمتكلمون ينكرون الكم مطلقاً فما معنى أصحية وجوده بالنسبة إلى الأين.
The second observatory - the first aim: Properties of quantity and its divisions. It has passed that this meaning includes continuous and discrete quantity. And with regard to actual division, it is separation and splitting, whether by cutting or breaking. And the first meaning of the properties of quantity: its occurrence in the body and the rest of the accidents, meaning the others, through the conjunction of quantity with them, for if you conceive something of them without considering with it number or magnitude, you could not suppose its division. And the second meaning: continuous quantity, which is magnitude, does not accept it, for the substrate remains with the accepted, otherwise it would not truly be a substrate necessarily. And upon splitting and separation occurring to the body, the quantity, i.e., the first magnitude, does not remain, because it is a single continuous thing in itself with no division in it at all, but rather it ceases and there occurs two quantities, i.e., two other magnitudes that were not actually existent, otherwise there would be in a single continuous thing infinite continuous things according to possible divisions. Yes, continuous quantity residing in corporeal matter prepares matter for accepting separative division, even if that quantity cannot coexist with that division. Upon it is the explanation, so it is not objected by what is said: 'There is no dispute about the existence of 'ayn, and the theologians deny quantity absolutely, so what is the meaning of its existence being more genuine relative to 'ayn?'
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage defines the properties of quantity and explains the first two. The author begins by stating the heading: 'The second observatory - the first aim: Properties of quantity and its divisions.' He then recalls that the meaning (of divisibility) applies to both continuous and discrete quantity. Actual division is of two types: separation (fasl) and splitting (fakk), whether by cutting or breaking. The first property of quantity is that it occurs in body and other accidents via the conjunction of quantity with them. To illustrate: if you conceive of something without number or magnitude, you cannot imagine its division. The second property is that continuous quantity (magnitude) does not accept splitting because the substrate must persist with what it accepts; otherwise it would not truly be a substrate. When splitting occurs to the body, the original magnitude ceases and two new magnitudes arise. If the original remained, there would be infinite magnitudes actually present in a single continuous thing, which is impossible. Nevertheless, continuous quantity in corporeal matter prepares matter for separative division, even if it cannot coexist with that division, analogous to how motion prepares for rest even though they cannot coexist. The author then notes that a potential objection is refuted by this explanation: the objection that there is no dispute about the existence of place ('ayn) and that theologians deny quantity absolutely, so how can quantity be more genuine? The response is that the explanation stands and the objection is not valid.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Splitting (fakk) is a property of continuous quantity itself.
Correction: The passage explains that splitting occurs in the matter, not in the magnitude. Continuous quantity does not accept splitting; rather, it prepares matter for it.

Key Takeaway
Continuous quantity makes things divisible but itself cannot be split; only matter undergoes splitting, preparing for divisibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Is there a pattern in your life where you try to 'split away' a sin while expecting your old self to remain unchanged? How does this metaphysical truth expose that either your old self must die or you are only fooling yourself?
?
The substrate must remain with what it receives. In your worship, your heart is the substrate—does it remain present during prayer, or does it cease and get replaced by distraction? What concrete action can you take today to ensure your heart stays with your ibadah until the end?
?
Al-Jurjani warns that if the original magnitude persisted after splitting, there would be infinite magnitudes in one thing. In what ways do you see yourself as a fragmented collection of desires and identities? Continuous and Discontinuous Quantity
Definition of continuous and discontinuous quantity with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental distinction between continuous and discontinuous quantity based on the presence or absence of a common boundary between parts.

Core Idea
Continuous quantity shares a boundary; discontinuous does not.

This passage introduces the primary classification of quantity in Islamic philosophy. The author defines continuous quantity as having a common boundary between its parts, like a line where the point is shared, and discontinuous quantity as lacking such a boundary, like the number ten when divided into six and four. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping more complex discussions about space, time, and number.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of continuous quantity
Read the first lines of the passage where the author defines continuous quantity using the example of a line and a point. Notice the figurative expression of the point as a 'part' of the line.
The point is not literally a part; it is a boundary.
2
Examine the example of discontinuous quantity
Read the example of the number ten. See how there is no common boundary between the six and the four parts. This contrasts with the line example.
The common boundary is absent in number.
3
Reflect on the importance of the common boundary
Ask yourself why the concept of a common boundary matters. It defines the unity and continuity of a quantity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد الثاني : الكم المتصل والفرضي. جزء المقدار لا يكون حداً مشتركاً بين جزئين آخرين منه فجعل النقطة جزءاً من الخط تجوز في العبارة ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يكن بين أجزائه حد مشترك (فالمنفصل كالعدد فإنك إن أشرت من العشرة إلى السادس مثلا انتهى إليه الستة وابتداء الأربعة الباقية من السابع لا منه ( أي من السادس ) فلم يكن ثمة أمر مشترك بينها ) يعني بين قسمي العشرة وهما الستة والأربعة كما كانت النقطة مشتركة بين قسمي الخط ( و ) الكم ) المتصل إما غير (قار أي لا يجوز اجتماع أجزائه المفروضة في الوجود ( وهو الزمان فالآن مشترك بين قسميه ( الماضي والمستقبل ) على نحو اشتراك النقطة بين قسمي الخط فيكون الزمان من قبيل الكم المتصل ) وإما قار الذات) أي يجوز اجتماع أجزائه المفروضة في الوجود ) وهو المقدار فإن انقسم) المقدار ( في الجهات الثلاث (فجسم) تعليمي وهو أتم المقادير ( أو في جهتين ) فقط ( فسطح أو في جهة واحدة ) فقط ( فخط) فهذه الأربعة أقسام للكم المتصل (و) الكم ( المنفصل هو العدد لا غير وذلك لأن قوام المنفصل بالمتفرقات والمتفرقات هي المفردات
Second Observatory - Second Objective: Continuous and Discontinuous Quantity. A part of magnitude does not have a common boundary between two other parts of it, so calling the point a part of the line is permissible in expression. (Otherwise), i.e., if there is no common boundary between its parts, then the discontinuous is like number, for if you point from ten to the sixth, for example, the six ends at it and the beginning of the remaining four is from the seventh, not from it (i.e., from the sixth), so there was no common thing between them, meaning between the two divisions of ten, which are six and four, just as the point was common between the two divisions of the line. And continuous quantity is either non-stationary, i.e., its assumed parts cannot coexist in existence, and it is time, for the 'now' is common between its two divisions (past and future) in the manner of the point's commonality between the two divisions of the line, so time is of the type of continuous quantity; or stationary in essence, i.e., its assumed parts can coexist in existence, and it is magnitude; then magnitude is divided into three dimensions, so it is a taught body and it is the most perfect of magnitudes; or into two dimensions only, so it is a surface; or into one dimension only, so it is a line. These are the four divisions of continuous quantity. And discontinuous quantity is only number, and that is because the discontinuous consists of discrete units, and the discrete units are the individuals.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage classifies quantity (al-kamm) into two main types: continuous (muttasil) and discontinuous (munfasil). It begins by defining continuous quantity as that which has a common boundary between its parts. The example given is a line, where the point serves as the common boundary between two segments of the line. Calling the point a 'part' of the line is a figurative expression, not a literal one. In contrast, discontinuous quantity lacks a common boundary, exemplified by number: when you divide ten into six and four, there is no common point between them—the six ends at the sixth unit and the four begins at the seventh. Continuous quantity is then subdivided based on whether its parts can coexist. Non-stationary (ghayr qārr) quantity, like time, has parts that do not coexist; the 'now' is the common boundary between past and future. Stationary (qārr al-dhāt) quantity, like magnitude, has parts that can coexist. Magnitude itself is further divided according to the number of dimensions: three dimensions yield a body (jism), two yield a surface (saṭḥ), and one yields a line (khaṭṭ). These are the four types of continuous quantity. Finally, the passage states that discontinuous quantity is exclusively number, because its essence is composed of discrete units (mutafarriqāt), which are the individual ones (mufradāt).

كم منفصل
حد مشترك

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The point is a literal part of the line.
Correction: The point is a common boundary, not a part with magnitude. It is figurative to call it a part.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Quantity is classified as continuous if its parts share a common boundary, and as discontinuous if they do not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you treat the 'boundaries' between different responsibilities or relationships as real points that separate and connect, or do you let them blur into a continuous mess? What is one step you can take today to establish a clear, common boundary where needed?
?
The point is not a part of the line, yet it defines the line’s division. Are there aspects of your faith that you mistake as 'parts' of your identity when they are actually boundaries that clarify your stance? How will you recalibrate your understanding?
?
When you divide your time between worldly and spiritual pursuits, do you see them as continuous (sharing a common goal) or discontinuous (completely separate)? How does the concept of a 'common boundary' challenge you to integrate them without mixing them?

Subdivisions of Continuous Quantity
Subdivision of continuous quantity into time and magnitude, and magnitude into body, surface, line.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main subdivisions of continuous quantity: non-stationary (time) and stationary (magnitude), and the further classification of magnitude by dimensions.

Core Idea
Continuous quantity is either time or magnitude; magnitude has three, two, or one dimension.

Having established the definition of continuous quantity, the author now divides it into two types based on whether its parts can coexist. Time, with its flowing 'now', is non-stationary, while magnitude is stationary and further divided into body, surface, and line according to the number of dimensions. This classification grounds discussions of physics and geometry.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of continuous quantity
Read the part where the author splits continuous quantity into non-stationary (ghayr qārr) and stationary (qārr al-dhāt). Understand the criterion: coexistence of parts.
Non-stationary: parts cannot coexist (time). Stationary: parts can coexist (magnitude).
2
Understand time as non-stationary
Note the example of time: the 'now' (ān) is common between past and future, similar to the point in a line.
Time is continuous but its parts do not exist simultaneously.
3
Study the subdivisions of magnitude
See how magnitude is divided by dimensions: three dimensions give a body, two give a surface, one gives a line. Recognize that these are taught (ta'līmī) bodies, i.e., mathematical.
These are ideal geometrical entities.
4
Contrast with discontinuous quantity
Finally, note that discontinuous quantity is only number, made of discrete units.

Intrinsic vs. Accidental Discontinuous Quantity
The author distinguishes intrinsic discontinuous quantity from accidental quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between quantity by essence (kamm bi-dhāt) and quantity by accident (kamm bi-l-‘araḍ) in discontinuous quantity, and why only the former is counted as a category.

Core Idea
Number is quantity by essence; collections of objects are quantity by accident.

This passage clarifies a subtle but important distinction within the category of discontinuous quantity. Not every grouping of ones yields the same kind of quantity; the key is how the units are considered. Let's read the text carefully to grasp the precise difference.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the technical terms 'كم بالذات' and 'كم بالعرض'.
The passage is compact; take your time.
2
Identify the two ways of considering 'one'
The passage says the 'one' can be taken either purely as a unit or as a specific thing that is one. Find where this distinction is made and how it leads to two types of quantity.
3
Understand why only intrinsic quantity is a category
The passage concludes that discussion focuses on quantity by essence because it is a proper category (maqūla). Reflect on why accidental quantity is not a category.
Only pure number, not collections of specific objects, is a fundamental category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمفردات آحاد والواحد إما أن يؤخذ من حيث هو واحد من غير أن يلاحظ معه شيء آخر أو يؤخذ من حيث إنه واحد هو شيء معين فالآحاد المأخوذة على الوجه الأول وحدات مجتمعات بينهما انفصال ذاتي فيكون عدداً مبلغه تلك الوحدات فهي كم بالذات والمأخوذة على الوجه الثاني أمور معروضة للوحدات منفصلة بانفصال الوحدات فهي كم بالعرض وإلى هذا المعنى أشار بقوله ( لأنه ) أي الكم المنفصل أن ر لا بد ينتهي إلى وحدات ( أي إلى آحاد كما عرفت ) والوحدة إن كانت نفس ذاتها ) أي نفس ذات تلك الآحاد بأن تكون مأخوذة من حيث إنها آحاد فقط ( فهو ) أي المجتمع من تلك الآحاد ( الكثرة ) التي هي العدد ( وإن ( كانت الوحدة ( عارضة لها أي لتلك الآحاد بأن تكون مأخوذة من حيث إنها أشياء معينة موصوفة بالوحدات ) فهي كم بالعرض والكلام في الكم بالذات )؛ لأنه الذي عد مقولة من المقولات .
And the individual units are ones, and the one is either taken insofar as it is one without considering anything else with it, or it is taken insofar as it is one [that] is a specific thing. The ones taken in the first aspect are units aggregated among which there is an essential separation, so it becomes number whose amount is those units, so they are quantity (kamm) by essence (bi-dhāt). And those taken in the second aspect are things that are accidental to the units, separated by the separation of the units, so they are quantity by accident (bi-l-‘araḍ). And to this meaning he referred by his statement 'because' (li-annahu), i.e., the discontinuous quantity must inevitably end in units (i.e., in ones as you know). And if the unity is the very essence of those ones, i.e., they are taken insofar as they are ones only, then it (i.e., the aggregate of those ones) is the multitude (al-kathra) which is number. And if the unity is accidental to them, i.e., to those ones, by being taken insofar as they are specific things described by unities, then they are quantity by accident, and the discussion is about quantity by essence, because it is what is counted as a category (maqūla) among the categories.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage explains the nature of discontinuous quantity, distinguishing between intrinsic (bi-dhāt) and accidental (bi-l-‘araḍ) quantity. It begins by stating that individual units (āḥād) are ones (wāḥidāt). A 'one' can be considered in two ways: (1) purely as one, without any additional consideration, or (2) as a specific thing that happens to be one. In the first case, the ones (āḥād) taken collectively are units (waḥidāt) that are essentially separate from each other (infiṣāl dhātī); the aggregate is number (al-‘adad) composed of those units, and this is quantity by essence (kamm bi-dhāt). In the second case, the ones are considered as specific objects that have the property of unity; they are separated by the separation of the units, but this separation is accidental, so they constitute quantity by accident (kamm bi-l-‘araḍ). The passage then references the author's earlier phrase 'because' (li-annahu), explaining that discontinuous quantity necessarily ends in units (wafqāt/āḥād). If the unity (al-waḥda) is the essence of those ones (i.e., considered only as ones), the aggregate is multitude (kathra) which is number. If unity is accidental to them (i.e., considered as specific things described by unity), then it is accidental quantity. The discussion, however, is focused on quantity by essence, because that is what is counted as one of the ten categories (maqūlāt).

Key Terms
كم بالذات
كم بالعرض
وحدة
عدد
كثرة
انفصال ذاتي
مقولة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All collections of distinct objects are the same kind of quantity.
Correction: Only when units are considered purely as units (abstract number) is it quantity by essence; when units are considered as specific objects, it is accidental quantity and not a category.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes quantity by essence (kamm bi-dhāt) from quantity by accident (kamm bi-l-‘araḍ) according to this passage?
A) Quantity by essence is continuous, quantity by accident is discontinuous
B) Quantity by essence arises from units taken purely as units, while quantity by accident arises from units taken as specific things
C) Quantity by essence is time, quantity by accident is magnitude
D) Quantity by essence is a category, quantity by accident is not

Key Takeaway
Discontinuous quantity is only a category when the units are considered purely as units, not as specific objects.

Definition of the Three Dimensions of a Body
Defines the three bodily dimensions with geometric order and notes polysemy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Islamic philosophers defined the three dimensions of a body (length, width, depth) and why distinguishing these technical meanings from everyday uses is crucial for avoiding error.

Core Idea
The three dimensions are defined by order of assumption and mutual perpendicularity.

In this passage, the author defines the three bodily dimensions with precise geometric relations. He then warns that the same words (length, width, depth) are used in other contexts, so clarity is essential. This lesson unpacks his definitions and highlights the danger of polysemy.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of length
Notice that length is defined as the first assumed extension. Think about how one might measure a body starting from one edge.
The term 'assumed' indicates a conceptual ordering.
2
Understand width and depth
Width is the second assumed extension, cutting length at right angles. Depth is third, cutting both at right angles. Visualize a corner with three perpendicular edges.
3
Appreciate the polysemy warning
The passage states that these terms have other meanings. Why is it important to distinguish? The commentary explains that this prevents error due to shared vocabulary.
This connects to the learning objective.
4
Apply the concepts
Try to identify the three dimensions in an ordinary object like a book. Which edge is length? Which is width? Which is depth?
This reinforces the definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثالث : الأبعاد الثلاثة للجسم. الأبعاد الثلاثة الجسمية تسمى الطول ( وهو الامتداد المفروض أولاً ) والعرض ) وهو الامتداد المفروض ثانياً : المقاطع للأول على زوايا قوائم (والعمق) وهو المفروض ثالثاً : المقاطع للأولين كذلك ( وإنها ) أي الطول والعرض والعمق ( تطلق على معان أخر سوى المعاني التي هي الأبعاد الثلاثة الجسمية ( فلا بد من الإشارة إليها أي إلى الأبعاد الجسمية والمعاني الأخر فإنه بين جميع ذلك ( ليحصل الأمن من الغلط الواقع بحسب اشتراك اللفظ وليتصور حقائقها )
Third Objective: The three dimensions of the body. The three bodily dimensions are called length, which is the extension assumed first; and width, which is the extension assumed second, cutting the first at right angles; and depth, which is assumed third, cutting the first two similarly. And that they (i.e., length, width, and depth) are used for other meanings besides the meanings that are the three bodily dimensions, so it is necessary to indicate them, i.e., the bodily dimensions and the other meanings, for he has made clear all of that in order to achieve security from the error occurring due to polysemy and to conceive their realities.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage defines the three dimensions of a body (al-ab‘ād al-thalātha al-jismiyya). It begins by naming them: length (ṭūl), width (‘arḍ), and depth (‘umq). Length is defined as the extension (imtidād) first assumed (al-mafrūḍ awwalan) in the body. Width is the extension assumed second, which cuts the first at right angles (qawā’im). Depth is the extension assumed third, cutting both the first two (al-awwalayn) similarly, i.e., at right angles. Then the passage notes that these three terms (length, width, depth) are also used for other meanings, not just for these bodily dimensions. Therefore, it is necessary to indicate which meaning is intended—the bodily dimensions or the other meanings—so that one can avoid error arising from polysemy (ishtirāk al-lafẓ) and correctly conceive the realities of these dimensions. The author has clarified all of this to ensure understanding and prevent confusion.

طول

امتداد
زاوية قائمة
اشتراك اللفظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The words length, width, and depth always refer to physical dimensions of a body.
Correction: In Arabic, these terms can also refer to non-physical extensions (e.g., length of time, breadth of knowledge). The passage warns that the bodily sense is one among many.

Checkpoint
How is depth (al-‘umq) defined in relation to the other dimensions?
A) It is the extension assumed first
B) It is the extension assumed second, cutting the first at right angles
C) It is the extension assumed third, cutting both length and width at right angles
D) It is the extension assumed arbitrarily

Key Takeaway
The three bodily dimensions are defined relationally by order of assumption and perpendicularity; awareness of polysemy prevents confusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, where might you be using terms like 'length', 'width', or 'depth' loosely without a clear, ordered definition? How could this lack of precision lead to misunderstanding in your thoughts about Allah's creation?
?
The passage warns that polysemy can cause error. Think of a concept in your faith that you assume everyone understands the same way. Are you willing to examine that assumption and define it clearly, even if it challenges you?
?
If your understanding of fundamental terms is built on undefined assumptions, what does that say about the strength of your beliefs? Will you commit to seeking precise definitions for the key terms of your aqeedah?

The Three Dimensions of the Body: Definitions and Homonymy
Definition of the three bodily dimensions with a warning about homonymy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definitions of the three bodily dimensions (length, width, depth) as ordered extensions intersecting at right angles, and why these terms can also refer to other meanings that must be distinguished to avoid confusion due to homonymy.

Core Idea
The three dimensions are defined by order and right-angle intersection, not by absolute length.

In this passage, the commentator lays out the technical definitions of the three dimensions of a body — length, width, and depth — as fundamental concepts in Islamic physics and metaphysics. He then cautions that these same words have other meanings in ordinary language, so careful distinction is needed to avoid error. This lesson will unpack those definitions and the problem of homonymy.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the three dimensions
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how each dimension is defined by its order of assumption and the condition of right-angle intersection.
The definitions are precise and geometric.
2
Understand the order and intersection conditions
Length is the first assumed extension, width is the second intersecting the first at right angles, depth is the third intersecting the first two at right angles. The order and perpendicularity are key.
This distinguishes them from common usage.
3
Note the warning about homonymy
The commentator states that these terms are also used for other meanings. Identify the warning and its purpose: to prevent error and conceive the true realities.
Homonymy (ishtirāk al-lafẓ) is a source of confusion.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Why does the commentator emphasize clarifying both sets of meanings? To avoid error and ensure proper understanding. Consider how this attention to terminology aids systematic theology.

Source Text

[ المقصد الثالث : الأبعاد الثلاثة للجسم ] الأبعاد الثلاثة الجسمية تسمى الطول ( وهو الامتداد المفروض أولاً ) والعرض ) وهو الامتداد المفروض ثانياً : المقاطع للأول على زوايا قوائم (والعمق) وهو المفروض ثالثاً : المقاطع للأولين كذلك ( وإنها ) أي الطول والعرض والعمق ( تطلق على معان أخر سوى المعاني التي هي الأبعاد الثلاثة الجسمية ( فلا بد من الإشارة إليها أي إلى الأبعاد الجسمية والمعاني الأخر فإنه بين جميع ذلك ( ليحصل الغلط الواقع بحسب اشتراك اللفظ وليتصور حقائقها ) أي حقائق معاني
[ Third Objective: The Three Dimensions of the Body ] The three dimensions of the body are called length (which is the extension assumed first), width (which is the extension assumed second, intersecting the first at right angles), and depth (which is the extension assumed third, intersecting the first two likewise). And they—i.e. length, width, and depth—are applied to other meanings besides the meanings which are the three bodily dimensions. So it is necessary to point to them—i.e. to the bodily dimensions and the other meanings—for he makes all that clear so that the error that occurs due to homonymy may be prevented, and so that their realities—i.e. the realities of the meanings—may be conceived.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the three dimensions of the body (al-abʿād ath-thalātha al-jismiyya). The commentator defines length (ṭūl) as the first assumed extension, width (ʿarḍ) as the second assumed extension intersecting the first at right angles, and depth (ʿumq) as the third assumed extension intersecting the first two at right angles. He then notes that these terms (length, width, depth) also have other meanings besides these specific geometric definitions. The commentator emphasizes the need to point out both the bodily dimensions and the other meanings to avoid confusion due to homonymy (ishtirāk al-lafẓ) and to properly conceive the realities.

Multiple Meanings of Length, Width, and Depth
The commentator enumerates the different meanings of length, width, and depth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the terms length, width, and depth have multiple meanings in classical Islamic philosophy, distinguishing geometric dimensions from broader uses.

Core Idea
Length, width, and depth are not univocal—they have different senses depending on context.

This passage from the commentary on al-Mawaqif clarifies a potential confusion. The terms length, width, and depth are used in multiple ways: some general, some specific to geometry. Understanding these distinctions is essential for reading classical texts on physics and theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is structured as a list of meanings for each term.
2
Identify the three terms and their sub-meanings
For each of length, width, and depth, list the different definitions given. Note that length has three meanings, width has three, and depth has one.
The commentary will help you distinguish the general from the geometric senses.
3
Compare with common understanding
Compare these multiple meanings with the everyday usage of these terms. Ask yourself: which meanings correspond to the geometric dimensions of a body, and which are more general?
The common misunderstanding is that they only have geometric meanings.
4
Reflect on the importance for kalam
Consider why a theologian would need to clarify these terms. How might ambiguity in length, width, and depth affect arguments about the physical world?
This connects to the need for precise language in theological reasoning.

المرصد الثاني - المقصد الثالث : الأبعاد الثلاثة للجسم هذه الألفاظ الثلاثة التي هي الطول والعرض والعمق ( أما الطول فيقال للامتداد الواحد ( مطلقاً) من غير أن يعتبر معه قيد وبهذا المعنى قيل : إنَّ كل خط فهو في نفسه طويل أي هو في نفسه بعد وامتداد واحد (و) يقال ( للامتداد المفروض أولاً) وهو أحد الأبعاد الجسمية كما ذكرناه (و) يقال (لأطول الامتدادين المتقاطعين في السطح وهذا هو المشهور فيما بين الجمهور ( وأما العرض فيقال للسطح) وهو ماله امتدادان وبهذا المعنى قيل : إِنَّ كل سطح فهو في نفسه عريض ( وللامتداد المفروض ثانياً) للمفروض أولا على قوائم كما ذكرناه وهو ثاني الأبعاد الجسمية ( وللامتداد الأقصر وأما العمق فيقال للامتداد الثالث ( المقاطع لكل واحد من الأولين على زوايا قائمة
Second Observatory - Third Objective: The Three Dimensions of the Body. These three terms which are length, width, and depth: As for length, it is said of the single extension absolutely, without any condition being considered with it, and in this sense it is said: every line is in itself long, i.e., it is in itself a single dimension and extension. And it is said of the extension assumed first, which is one of the bodily dimensions as we have mentioned. And it is said of the longer of the two intersecting extensions in a surface, and this is well-known among the common people. As for width, it is said of the surface, which has two extensions, and in this sense it is said: every surface is in itself wide. And it is said of the extension assumed second to the first at right angles as we have mentioned, and it is the second of the bodily dimensions. And it is said of the shorter extension. As for depth, it is said of the third extension intersecting each of the first two at right angles.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the different meanings of the three terms length (ṭūl), width (ʿarḍ), and depth (ʿumq). The commentator lists multiple usages to clarify potential confusion due to homonymy. For length: (1) any single extension without qualification, (2) the first assumed extension (bodily dimension), (3) the longer of two intersecting extensions in a surface. For width: (1) a surface (something with two extensions), (2) the second assumed extension (bodily dimension), (3) the shorter extension. For depth: the third extension intersecting the first two at right angles (the third bodily dimension). This classification helps distinguish the specific geometric meanings from broader usages.

Key Terms

العرض
العمق
الامتداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that the only meanings of length, width, and depth are the geometric dimensions, when they also have general meanings (e.g., any line is 'long' simply because it has one dimension).
Correction: The passage shows that each term has multiple senses: a general sense (e.g., any line is long) and a specific geometric sense (e.g., the first assumed dimension of a body).

Key Takeaway
Length, width, and depth each have specialized geometric senses that differ from their general usage, and distinguishing these is crucial for precise theological and philosophical discussion.

Initial Meanings of Length, Width, and Depth
Defines the three body dimensions and gives initial meanings for length, width, depth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three primary meanings of length, three of width, and one of depth, and how these definitions form the basis for describing bodily dimensions in kalam.

Core Idea
Length, width, and depth each have multiple technical meanings in kalam, not just one.

This passage from al-Mawaqif establishes the technical definitions of the three spatial dimensions. Before we can discuss the nature of bodies, we must clarify what we mean by length, width, and depth. The text presents multiple valid usages, some absolute and some relative, which are essential for precise theological and philosophical discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the number of meanings given for each dimension.
Notice that length has three distinct definitions.
2
Identify the meanings for each dimension
List the meanings: length (absolute extension, first assumed extension, longer intersecting extension), width (surface, second assumed extension, shorter extension), depth (third extension at right angles).
3
Reflect on the absolute vs. relative distinction
Observe that some meanings are absolute (every line is long) while others are relative (longer of two). This nuance is key in kalam arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد الثالث : الأبعاد الثلاثة للجسم
هذه الألفاظ الثلاثة التي هي الطول والعرض والعمق ( أما الطول فيقال للامتداد الواحد ( مطلقاً) من غير أن يعتبر معه قيد وبهذا المعنى قيل : إنَّ كل خط فهو في نفسه طويل أي هو في نفسه بعد وامتداد واحد (و) يقال ( للامتداد المفروض أولاً) وهو أحد الأبعاد الجسمية كما ذكرناه (و) يقال (لأطول الامتدادين المتقاطعين في السطح وهذا هو المشهور فيما بين الجمهور ( وأما العرض فيقال للسطح) وهو ماله امتدادان وبهذا المعنى قيل : إِنَّ كل سطح فهو في نفسه عريض ( وللامتداد المفروض ثانياً) للمفروض أولا على قوائم كما ذكرناه وهو ثاني الأبعاد الجسمية ( وللامتداد الأقصر وأما العمق فيقال للامتداد الثالث ( المقاطع لكل واحد من الأولين على زوايا قائمة
قوله : ( أي حقائق معاني إلخ) يعني أنَّ الكلام على حذف المضاف أو التجوز بإقامة الدال مقام المدلول أو الاستخدام.
قوله : ( للامتداد الواحد ) أي الذاهب في جهة واحدة. قوله : ( وبهذا المعنى قيل إنَّ كل خط الخ ) ما ذكره الشارح قدس سره مع كونه غير صحيح في نفسه إذ المناسب لهذه المعاني الثلاثة أن يقال : كل خط في نفسه طول وكل سطح عرض وكل جسم عمق مخالف لما في الشفاء حيث قال : وبعض هذه عوارض خاصة للكمية كالطول والقصر الذي بالقياس مثل ما يقال : إِنَّ هذا الخط طويل والآخر ليس بطويل بل قصير وإن كان كل خط طويلاً في نفسه بمعنى آخر أي من حيث له بعد واحد وهذا سطح عريض وذلك الآخر ليس بعريض بل ضيق وإن كان كل سطح عريضاً في نفسه بمعنى آخر أي من حيث له مع بعد يفرض طولاً بعد يفرض عرضاً ويقال : هذا الجسم ثخين والآخر رقيق وليس بثخين وإن كان كل جسم له ثخين بمعنى آخر أي من حيث له عمق أي من حيث له ثلاثة أبعاد انتهى فإنه صريح في أن إطلاق الطويل على الخط من حيث أنه ذو امتداد لا من حيث أنه بعد والعريض على السطح باعتبار أن له بعداً مفروضاً ثانياً والعميق باعتبار أنَّ له بعداً ثالثاً بل أبعاداً ثلاثة .
قوله : ( للامتداد المفروض أولاً) وإن كان قصيراً كطول البرج. قوله : ( وهو أحد الأبعاد الخ تذكير لما علم سابقاً . )
قوله : ( لأطول الامتدادين) في الشفاء من غير أن يعتبر تقدم وتأخر.
قوله: (وهو ماله امتدادان) في الشفاء العرض يقال للذي فيه بعدان .
قوله : ( وبهذا المعنى إن كل خط فهو في نفسه طويل الامتداد الذي أطلق عليه الطول إِنَّ حمل على المعنى المصدري فوجه إطلاق الطويل على الخطوط ظاهر ويكون معنى كلام الشارح أي هو في نفسه بعد واحد وامتداد واحد أنه ذو امتداد واحد وإن حمل على الممتد كما يطلق الاتصال الجوهري على نفسه الصورة الجسمية والاتصال العرضي على المقدار فيكون معنى قولهم : كل خط طويل مع أن الظاهر أن يقال : كل خط طول أنه طويل بطول هو نفسه كما يقال : الضوء مضيء.
The Second Observatory - The Third Objective: The Three Dimensions of the Body. These three terms are length, width, and depth. (As for length, it is said of the single extension (absolutely) without considering any condition, and in this sense it is said: every line is in itself long, i.e., it is in itself a single dimension and extension. (And) it is said (of the extension assumed first), which is one of the bodily dimensions as we mentioned. (And) it is said (of the longer of the two intersecting extensions on a surface, and this is what is well-known among the majority). (As for width, it is said of a surface), which has two extensions, and in this sense it is said: every surface is in itself wide. (And of the extension assumed second) to that assumed first at right angles as we mentioned, and it is the second of the bodily dimensions. (And of the shorter extension). As for depth, it is said of the third extension (that cuts each of the first two at right angles). His saying: (i.e., the realities of meanings etc.) means that the speech involves deletion of the genitive or metonymy by making the signifier stand for the signified, or usage. His saying: (for the single extension) i.e., going in one direction. His saying: (and in this sense it is said that every line etc.) What the commentator (may his soul be sanctified) mentioned, despite being incorrect in itself—for the appropriate thing for these three meanings is to say: every line is in itself length, every surface width, and every body depth—contradicts what is in al-Shifa, where he said: Some of these are specific accidents of quantity like length and shortness by comparison, such as it is said: this line is long and another is not long but short, although every line is long in itself in another sense, i.e., in that it has a single dimension; and this surface is wide and that other is not wide but narrow, although every surface is wide in itself in another sense, i.e., in that it has, along with a dimension assumed as length, a dimension assumed as width; and it is said: this body is thick and another is not thick, although every body has thickness in another sense, i.e., in that it has depth, i.e., in that it has three dimensions. End of quote. It is explicit that the application of 'long' to the line is from the aspect that it has extension, not from the aspect that it is a dimension; and 'wide' to the surface is by virtue of having a second assumed dimension; and 'deep' is by virtue of having a third dimension, nay, three dimensions. His saying: (for the extension assumed first) even if it is short like the length of a tower. His saying: (and it is one of the bodily dimensions etc.) is a reminder of what was known earlier. His saying: (for the longer of the two extensions) in al-Shifa without considering priority and posteriority. His saying: (and it has two extensions) in al-Shifa: width is said for that which has two dimensions. His saying: (and in this sense, every line is in itself long—the extension that is called 'length'—if it is taken in the verbal noun sense, then the application of 'long' to lines is apparent, and the meaning of the commentator's speech, i.e., it is in itself a single dimension and a single extension, is that it has a single extension; and if it is taken as the extended thing, just as essential connection is applied to the corporeal form itself and accidental connection to quantity, then the meaning of their saying 'every line is long'—while the apparent thing would be to say 'every line is length'—is that it is long by a length that is itself, just as it is said: light is luminous.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage defines the three dimensions of the body—length, width, and depth—and provides their primary meanings. It begins by stating that these three terms are the subject. For length, three meanings are given: (1) a single absolute extension, in which sense every line is considered long; (2) the extension assumed first in a body, which is one of the bodily dimensions; (3) the longer of two intersecting extensions on a surface, which is the common understanding among the majority. For width, it is said of a surface which has two extensions; also of the extension assumed second at right angles to the first, which is the second bodily dimension; and also of the shorter extension. For depth, it is said of the third extension that cuts the first two at right angles. The commentary then explains these definitions with glosses. It notes that the phrase 'realities of meanings' indicates either a deletion or a metonymy. The commentator critiques the statement that every line is long in itself, arguing that the correct statement should be that every line is length, every surface width, and every body depth. He contrasts this with Ibn Sina's view in al-Shifa, where 'long' and 'short' are relative accidents, but every line is long in the sense of having a single dimension, every surface wide in having two dimensions, and every body thick in having three dimensions. The commentator clarifies that the application of 'long' to the line is based on its having extension, not on being a dimension itself. Further glosses explain that the first assumed extension can be short (like a tower's height), and that in al-Shifa, the longer of two extensions is considered without priority. The commentary concludes with a linguistic note about whether 'long' is derived from the verbal noun 'length' or from the extended thing, analogizing with 'light is luminous.'

Key Terms

العرض
العمق
الامتداد
الخط

الجسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'long' just means having a great measurement, but in kalam it has three distinct technical senses.
Correction: Length in kalam can refer to absolute single extension (so every line is long), the first assumed dimension of a body, or the longer of two intersecting extensions. Context determines the intended meaning.

Checkpoint

A) One

Key Takeaway
Length, width, and depth are defined with multiple technical meanings, emphasizing that spatial dimensions are understood both absolutely and relatively in kalam.

Depth and Its Multiple Meanings in Kalam
Enumerates meanings of depth, thickness, and additional spatial dimension definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various technical meanings of depth (عمق) and the additional meanings of length, width, and depth as classified in kalam logic.

Core Idea
Depth is more than thickness—it has ascending and descending forms and four distinct meanings.

This passage completes the definitions of depth and introduces further meanings for all three spatial dimensions. It clarifies that depth can be understood as thickness, as the fill between surfaces, and as a restricted dimension (descending vs ascending). It then provides additional, less common meanings for length, width, and depth, and notes which are pure quantities.

Guided Path
1
Understand the primary definitions
Read the first part defining depth as the third bodily dimension and as thickness, the fill between surfaces.
Note that thickness is equated with the mathematical body.
2
Distinguish descending vs ascending thickness
Depth can be restricted by considering descent (as in well depth) or ascent (as in minaret thickness). These are called depth and thickness respectively.
3
Identify the additional meanings
The author gives a fourth meaning for length (center to circumference), a fifth for length (head to foot), a fourth for width (right to left), and a fourth for depth (chest to back).
These are body-relative extensions.
4
Recognize the pure quantities
Some of these meanings are pure mathematical quantities: length as a line, width as a surface, depth as thickness.
This classification ties back to geometry.
5
Review the commentary glosses
The commentary clarifies that 'fill between surfaces' refers to an imaginary void, and that the mention of a single surface is just an example.
It also explains why 'fill' is not the verbal noun but the filled thing.

His saying: (that is bounded by a (single) surface like a solid sphere) he indicated by that that the mention of the surface is by way of example, since the aim is not to give a comprehensive and restrictive definition, but merely to apply to the meanings. His saying: (the single extension which is the line) he stated here that the first meaning is the line itself, and it indicates. His saying: (fill between the surfaces i.e., the mathematical body, etc.) Since the expression of the text apparently does not encompass the mathematical body that is in a solid sphere, for it has one surface, not surfaces, and encompasses the natural body, the commentator interpreted it explicitly as intended, although his taking it from the author's expression is forced. If he had substituted 'i.e.' with 'meaning', it would be more apparent. Furthermore, what is in his phrase 'fill between the surfaces' is either an intensified indefinite or a relative, and the addition of 'fill' to it is explanatory. On both estimates, what is meant by 'fill' is neither the verbal noun nor the resultant thing as was thought, but the filled thing either terminologically or by linguistic metonymy.

Commentary
This passage completes the definitions of depth and adds further meanings for all three dimensions. It first returns to depth, stating it is also called thickness, which is the fill between surfaces, i.e., the mathematical body bounded by one or more surfaces. In this sense, any body is inherently deep. Depth can also be restricted as descending thickness (called depth) or ascending thickness (called thickness/samk), as in depth of a well and thickness of a minaret. The passage then provides additional meanings for length, width, and depth beyond the first three. For length: (4) the extension from the center of the universe to its circumference; (5) the extension from a human's head to foot or from a quadruped's head to rear. For width: (4) the extension from right to left. For depth: (4) the extension from chest to back or from back to ground. The passage concludes by noting that some of these meanings are pure quantities: length as the single extension (the line), width as the surface, and depth as thickness. The commentary glosses each part, explaining that 'fill between surfaces' in al-Shifa refers to the imaginary void filled, and that the mention of a single surface is an example. It notes that the author did not combine the additional meanings with the earlier ones, implying many applications. The commentary also clarifies that the first meaning of length is the line itself, and discusses the interpretation of 'fill'—whether it is a verbal noun or metaphorical.

Correction: Depth has at least four meanings in kalam: as a dimension, as thickness, as descending thickness, and as chest-to-back extension. Length and width also have multiple technical meanings.

The First Argument Against Number: Composition from Non-Existent Units
Al-Jurjani presents the first proof against number and the philosophical response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutakallimun argue that number is impossible because it is composed of non-existent units, and how the philosophers respond by identifying the unity of a unit with the unit itself.

Core Idea
Number is impossible if its parts are non-existent, but the unit's unity is itself the unit.

This passage presents a classic debate between the mutakallimun and philosophers over the reality of number. The mutakallimun argue that number, being composed of units, cannot exist because each unit would require an infinite regress of unities. The philosophers counter with the doctrine that a thing's unity is identical to the thing itself. Understanding this exchange is crucial for grasping the Islamic discourse on the reality of mathematical entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to get the overall structure of the argument.
Notice the parentheses indicate embedded clauses; the argument is dense but clear.
2
Identify the mutakallimun's claim
The passage says the mutakallimun deny number (discrete quantity) because number is composed of units, and units are not existent. Find this statement.
The claim is: number composed of non-existent units must be non-existent.
3
Examine the proof of non-existence of the unit
The passage gives two matters to prove the unit does not exist externally; the first is given here: if the unit existed, it would have a unity, leading to an infinite regress.
Understand why this is problematic: each unity would need another unity.
4
Study the philosophers' response
The philosophers answer that the unity of the unity is itself the unity, analogous to the existence of existence being identical to existence. This stops the regress.
This is a key metaphysical principle: identity of a thing and its unity.
5
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how identifying unity with the thing itself resolves the infinite regress. Does this fully answer the mutakallimun's objection?
The commentator notes this argument has been discussed earlier, implying it is a standard move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنَّ المتكلمين أنكروا العدد الذي هو الكم المنفصل (خلافاً للحكماء لمسلكين أحدهما أنه أي العدد الذي هو الكثرة ) مركب من الوحدات والوحدة ليست وجودية وعدم الجزء يستلزم عدم الكل ضرورة) فالعدد المركب من الوحدات العدمية يكون عدمياً قطعاً ( بيان أنَّ الوحدة لا توجد ) في الخارج ( أمران : الأول لو وجدت الوحدة فلها (وحدة لأن كل موجود موصوف بأنه واحد ( ولزم التسلسل) في الوحدات المترتبة الموجودة معاً (قالوا) أي الحكماء في الجواب : ( وحدة الوحدة نفس الوحدة ) على قياس ما قيل في وجود الوجود ( وقد مر ) هذا النوع الاستدلال مع جوابه فيما سبق
Indeed, the mutakallimun denied number, which is the discrete quantity, contrary to the philosophers, on two paths: one of them being that number, which is plurality, is composed of units, and the unit is not existent; and the non-existence of the part necessitates the non-existence of the whole necessarily. So the number composed of non-existent units is non-existent certainly. [This is] an explanation that the unit does not exist in the external world through two matters: the first: if the unit existed, it would have a unity, because every existent is described as one, and an infinite regress would follow in the arranged units existing together. They said — meaning the philosophers in response: the unity of the unity is itself the unity, analogous to what was said about the existence of existence; and this type of reasoning with its answer has already passed earlier.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two arguments (مسلكين) used by the mutakallimun to deny the reality of number as a discrete quantity. The argument runs: number is a plurality composed of units; units are not existent (for reasons to be given); therefore number, whose parts are non-existent, is itself non-existent. To prove that units are non-existent, two proofs are offered. The first proof is the one here: if a unit existed in the external world, it would itself be one and would require another unity, leading to an infinite regress of unities. The philosophers answer that the unity of a unit is not a separate attribute but identical with the unit itself — just as the existence of existence is not a separate existence. This stops the regress. The commentator notes that this argument has been discussed previously.

Key Terms

الوحدة (unit/unity)
التسلسل (infinite regress)
وحدة الوحدة (unity of unity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the philosophers are saying unity is a property that requires another unity, thus accepting the regress.
Correction: The text states 'وحدة الوحدة نفس الوحدة' — the unity of the unit is itself the unit, so no further unity is needed; the regress stops.

Checkpoint

B) They say the unity of a unit is itself the unit, so no further unity is needed.

D) They accept the regress and argue it is infinite.

Key Takeaway
The first argument against number hinges on the nature of unity; the philosophers save number by identifying unity with the existent itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What would it mean for you to accept that your core self is simply itself, without needing further proof?
?
The philosophers responded that the unity of the unit is the unit itself. When you think about the oneness of Allah (tawheed), have you ever felt the need to 'break down' His oneness into attributes, only to feel lost? How does the insight that His unity is identical with His essence free you from a restless search for a deeper foundation?

The Mutakallimun's Denial of Time: First Reason
The commentator presents the first reason for denying time: its parts are successive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first reason the mutakallimun used to deny the existence of time: that its parts are necessarily successive and cannot coexist.

Core Idea
The mutakallimun deny time because if its parts coexisted, past and present would be simultaneous — an absurdity.

The mutakallimun were known for denying many philosophical concepts, including time. In this passage, the commentator presents their first argument: that time, if real, would have parts like yesterday and today that must be successive. We will examine this reasoning and see why they concluded time cannot be a real entity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the English translation. Notice the dense style of the commentary.
2
Identify the key claim about time's parts
The passage asserts that time's parts (yesterday, today) are successive. Understand that 'successive' means they cannot exist together.
The term 'non-quiescent quantity' (كم متصل غير قار) is key.
3
Understand the reductio argument
If time had a quiescent essence (its parts coexisting), then the flood event would be simultaneous with today's events, which is absurd. This forces the conclusion that time's parts must be successive.
4
Distinguish kinds of priority
The commentator clarifies that the priority of yesterday over today is not like causal priority (where cause and effect coexist). Temporal priority does not allow coexistence.
This distinction is crucial to the argument.
5
Reflect on the equality of time's parts
All parts of time are equal in reality, so no part naturally depends on another. This removes any possibility of a natural order, further supporting the denial of time's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد السابع : إنكار المتكلمين للزمن الجزء ( أنهم ) أعني المتكلمين كما أنكروا العدد والمقدار الذي هو الكم المتصل القار ( أنكروا أيضاً ( الزمان ) الذي هو الكم المتصل غير القار ( لوجهين . الأول : أنَّ الزمان ) على تقدير كونه موجوداً ( أمسه مقدم على يومه إذ لا يجوز أن يكون الزمان قار الذات وإلا لكان الحادث في زمان الطوفان حادثاً اليوم وبالعكس، وهو باطل بالضرورة، بل يجب أن تكون أجزاؤه ممتنعة الاجتماع ) وليس) تقدم أمسه على يومه ( تقدماً بالعلية والذات ) أي الطبع ( والشرف والرتبة ) لأن المتقدم بهذه الوجوه يجامع المتأخر في الوجود وليس الأمس مما يمكن اجتماعه مع اليوم وأيضاً أجزاء الزمان متساوية في الحقيقة فلا يكون احتياج بعضها إلى بعض أولى من عكسه
The seventh topic: The Denial of Time by the Mutakallimun. Section: (They), meaning the mutakallimun, just as they denied number and magnitude which is continuous quiescent quantity, (they also denied time) which is continuous non-quiescent quantity, (for two reasons. The first: that time) on the assumption of its existence, (its yesterday is prior to its today, because it is not permissible for time to be quiescent in essence, otherwise the event in the time of the flood would be happening today, and vice versa, and that is absurd by necessity; rather, its parts must be impossible of coexistence) and (the priority of its yesterday over its today is not a priority by causality and essence) i.e., nature (and honor and rank, because that which is prior in these ways coexists with the posterior in existence, but yesterday is not something that can coexist with today; also, the parts of time are equal in reality, so the need of some for others is not more appropriate than the opposite).
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents the first reason used by the mutakallimun to deny the existence of time. They argue that time, if existent, would have parts that are necessarily successive: yesterday is prior to today. If time were quiescent (qār al-dhāt), meaning its parts exist simultaneously, then the event of the flood would be simultaneous with today's events, which is absurd. Therefore, time's parts must be impossible of coexistence. Moreover, this priority is not by causality, nature, honor, or rank, because things prior in those ways can coexist with the posterior (e.g., a cause coexists with its effect). But yesterday and today cannot coexist. Additionally, all parts of time are equal in reality, so one part's dependence on another is not more justified than the reverse, preventing any natural priority.

Key Terms
زمان
كم متصل غير قار
قار الذات
تقدم
أمس
يوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Temporal priority is like causal priority, where cause and effect can coexist.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that temporal priority (yesterday before today) does not allow coexistence, unlike causal priority.

Checkpoint
Why do the mutakallimun deny the existence of time according to the first reason?
A) Because time's parts are equal in essence
B) Because if time were quiescent, past and present events would be simultaneous
C) Because time is a non-quiescent quantity
D) Because priority by causality allows coexistence

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun's first reason to deny time is that its parts must be successive, and this succession cannot be explained by causal priority or natural order, so time cannot be a real entity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if time is a real container for your actions, or do you recognize that only the present moment is real and that your past moments are gone forever? How does this understanding affect your urgency to obey Allah now?
?
If time's parts cannot coexist, then your past sins are irrevocably gone — are you using that truth to move forward in repentance, or are you still chained to the past as if it were present?
?
The mutakallimun argued that time cannot be real because its parts are successive. How does this impact your view of Allah's eternal nature, which transcends time? Do you truly believe that Allah is not bound by time, or do you still imagine Him as existing within a timeline?
