Defining Dryness (Yubūsa): Two Interpretations and al-Razi's Alternative
Defines yubūsa via two interpretations of ruṭūba and presents al-Razi's alternative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how dryness (yubūsa) is defined through two interpretations of wetness (ruṭūba) and how Imam al-Razi refined the definition to distinguish it from solidity (ṣalāba).

Core Idea
Dryness is not just the opposite of wetness; it is a quality of easy separation and difficult connection.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif delves into the precise definition of dryness (yubūsa) by considering two contrasting interpretations of its counterpart, wetness (ruṭūba). It then presents Imam al-Razi's alternative definition, which uses empirical examples to differentiate dryness from crumbliness and solidity. Understanding these nuances is crucial for grasping Islamic natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two definitions based on wetness
The passage first gives two definitions of dryness corresponding to two definitions of wetness. Read those carefully.
The first definition of wetness leads to dryness as difficulty of adhesion and separation; the second leads to difficulty of shaping and leaving shape.
2
Examine al-Razi's alternative classification
Imam al-Razi proposes a different approach: he classifies bodies based on ease/difficulty of separation and connection, distinguishing dry (yābis), crumbly (hashsh), and viscous (luzj).
Notice that al-Razi attributes this classification to Ibn Qurra al-Thabit.
3
Appreciate the distinction from solidity
The commentary explains why al-Razi's definition is better: it avoids conflating dryness with solidity (ṣalāba), which is resistance to pressure.
This differentiation is the key doctrinal point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الثاني : في الرطوبة واليبوسة
وثالثها أن اليبوسة تقابل الرطوبة اتفاقاً (فهي إما عسر الالتصاق ) والانفصال أي كيفية تقتضي عسرهما على التفسير الأول للرطوبة . ( أو عسر التشكل وتركه أي كيفية تقتضي ذلك على التفسير الثاني لها . قال الإمام الرازي: لعل الأقرب في بيان حقيقة اليابس أن يقال : ( من الأجسام التي نشاهدها (ما يسهل تفرقه ويصعب اتصاله إما لذاته بأن يكون ذلك الجسم في نفسه بحيث تتفرق أجزاؤه وتنفرك بسهولة ( وهو اليابس ) . فاليبوسة حينئذ هي الكيفية التي يكون الجسم بها سهل التفرق عسر الاجتماع ( وإما اللحامات ( سهلة الانفراك (بين أجزائه) الصغيرة ( الصلبة) التي يكون كل واحد منها عسر التفرق في نفسه (وهو الهش ومنها ما هو بالعكس مما ذكر ( فيسهل اتصاله ويصعب تفرقه وهو اللزج قال : وهذا ما وجدته في مباحث ابن قرة الثابت) هذا الكلام منقول من المباحث المشرقية وليس فيه ذكر اللزج في التقسيم المنسوب إلى الثابت والمذكور في الملخص أن من
قوله: (لأنه متصل واحد في الحقيقة إلخ) في كون السيال متصلاً واحداً في الحقيقة نظر
لجواز أن يكون السيلان سبباً للانفكاك بين الأجزاء نعم الماء الراكد متصل .
قوله : ( لعل الأقرب إلخ) لعل وجه الأقربية أنه قال : أولاً لو فسرنا اليبوسة بالكيفية التي باعتبارها يعسر قبول الأشكال . لم يبق بينها وبين الصلابة فرق . ثم قال بعد نقل ما قاله ابن قرة فظهر الفرق بين اليبس والهشاشة وبين الصلابة. وأنت خبير بوضوح الفرق لأن الصلابة كيفية بها ممانعة الغامز وأين هذا من اليبوسة؟
The third observation - the second intention: on wetness and dryness. And the third of them: that dryness is opposite to wetness by agreement. (So it is either difficulty of adhesion and separation) i.e. a quality that necessitates their difficulty according to the first interpretation of wetness. (Or difficulty of shaping and leaving it) i.e. a quality that necessitates that according to the second interpretation of it. Imam al-Razi said: perhaps the closest in explaining the reality of the dry is to say: (Among the bodies that we observe (are those that are easy to separate and difficult to connect, either in itself by that body being such that its parts separate and crumble easily (and that is the dry). So dryness then is the quality by which the body is easy to separate and difficult to unite. (And sometimes [it is] the welds (easy to crumble (between its small (solid) parts (each of which is difficult to separate in itself (and that is the crumbly. And among them is that which is the opposite of what was mentioned (so it is easy to connect and difficult to separate, and it is the viscous. He said: this is what I found in the investigations of Ibn Qurra al-Thabit. This speech is quoted from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya, and there is no mention of the viscous in the division attributed to al-Thabit; and it is mentioned in al-Mulakhkhas that among) His saying: (because it is one continuous substance in reality etc.) regarding the flowing being one continuous substance in reality there is consideration, for it is possible that flowing is a cause of disconnection between parts; yes, still water is continuous. His saying: (perhaps the closest etc.) perhaps the aspect of closeness is that he said: first, if we defined dryness as the quality by which it is difficult to accept shapes, no difference would remain between it and solidity. Then he said after quoting what Ibn Qurra said: so the difference appeared between dryness and crumbliness and between solidity. And you are well aware of the clarity of the difference, because solidity is a quality by which there is resistance to the presser, and where is this from dryness?
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the term 'yubūsa' (dryness). It begins by stating that dryness is the opposite of wetness (ruṭūba) by consensus. Then it presents two possible definitions of dryness based on two different interpretations of wetness. According to the first interpretation of wetness (as ease of adhesion and difficulty of separation), dryness is defined as difficulty of adhesion and separation, i.e., a quality that makes it hard for parts to stick together or separate. According to the second interpretation of wetness (as ease of shaping and leaving the shape), dryness is defined as difficulty of shaping and leaving the shape, i.e., a quality that makes it hard to form a shape or retain it. Imam al-Razi then proposes an alternative that he considers closer to the truth: he defines the dry (al-yābis) as a body whose parts easily separate and are difficult to connect, either inherently (like a stone that crumbles easily) or due to weak welds between solid parts (which is the crumbly, al-hashsh). The opposite is the viscous (al-luzj), which is easy to connect and hard to separate. Al-Razi attributes this classification to Ibn Qurra al-Thabit. The commentator notes that this passage is taken from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya, and that the division attributed to al-Thabit does not include the viscous, while al-Mulakhkhas mentions it. The commentary then addresses two issues: first, a consideration regarding flowing bodies being one continuous substance in reality (since flow might cause disconnection, unlike still water which is continuous). Second, an explanation of why al-Razi's definition is closer: if dryness were defined as difficulty of accepting shapes, it would be indistinguishable from solidity (ṣalāba). By providing the distinction through Ibn Qurra's classification, the difference between dryness, crumbliness, and solidity becomes clear: solidity is resistance to pressure, which is not the same as dryness.

الرطوبة
اليابس
الهش
اللزج
الصلابة
الإمام الرازي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dryness (yubūsa) is the same as solidity (ṣalāba).
Correction: Dryness is a quality of easy separation and difficulty of connection, while solidity is resistance to pressure. They are distinct.

Checkpoint
According to the first interpretation of wetness, how is dryness defined?
A) Difficulty of shaping and leaving it
B) Difficulty of adhesion and separation
C) Ease of separation and difficulty of cohesion
D) Resistance to pressure

Key Takeaway
Dryness is defined as the quality of easy separation and difficult connection, distinct from solidity, and can be understood through two interpretations of wetness or al-Razi's empirical classification.

The Problem with the Initial Definition of Dryness
First objection to the initial definition of dryness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of dryness as easy separation and difficult cohesion leads to counterintuitive consequences.

Core Idea
Definitions must match tangible reality, not produce absurdities.

The commentator begins by examining a definition of dryness (yubūsa) as the quality of being easily separated and difficult to reunite. This definition, however, leads to several surprising results that challenge our common sense. Let's explore these problems and the suggestion that perhaps a different term is meant.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the commentator's first objection.
2
Identify the counterintuitive consequences
Notice that according to this definition, stone would not be dry and fire would be wet. Why does the commentator think this is a problem?
3
Consider the suggested reinterpretation
The commentator suggests that perhaps 'dryness' here means 'aridity' (jafāf), not the philosophical quality. How does this help?
4
Reflect on the lesson
Think about how definitions in theology must align with our experience and common understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فاليبوسة حينئذ هي الكيفية إلخ ) على هذا لا تكون اليبوسة من الملموسات ولا يكون الحجر يابساً ويكون النار رطباً، لأنه وإن كانت سهلة التفرق لكنها ليست عسيرة الاجتماع أو يكون واسطة ولعل هذه اليبوسة بمعنى الجفاف. فإن الجسم المبتل إذا أثر فيه الرطوبة الغريبة يصعب تفرقه ويسهل اجتماعه عما كان قبله . وإذا جف صار الأمر بالعكس.
قوله : ( في التقسيم المنسوب إلخ) لكن ذكره الإمام في فصل بيان الهشاشة واللزوجة .
قوله: (والمذكور إلخ) يعني اكتفى في تفسيرها بسهولة الانفراك وعدمها وجعل مقابل الهش واليابس، الصلب ولا يخفى أنه ليس مقابلا لهما .
قوله : ( فهي إما عسر الالتصاق والانفصال إلخ) قيل : فعلى هذا يكون بينهما واسطة إذ ما يعسر به واحد منها، ويسهل الآخر فهو لا رطب ولا يابس ولهذا قال الإمام : هذا التعريف بالصلابة أجدر .
قوله : ( أو عسر التشكل وتركه يرد على هذا التعريف بأنه صادق على الصلابة الموجودة عند الفلاسفة اللهم إلا أن يثبت استلزام الصلابة لليبوسة وإن ذلك العسر في الجسم الصلب لأجل يبوسته لا لأجل صلابته وأنى ذلك الإثبات ؟
His saying: (So dryness then is the quality etc.) According to this, dryness is not among the tangible things, and stone would not be dry, and fire would be wet, because although it is easily divisible, it is not difficult to unite, or there would be an intermediate. Perhaps this dryness means aridity. For a moist body, when affected by extraneous wetness, becomes difficult to separate and easy to unite compared to its previous state. And when it dries, the opposite occurs. His saying: (In the division attributed etc.) But the Imam mentioned it in the chapter on explaining crumbliness and viscosity. His saying: (And it is mentioned etc.) means he sufficed in its interpretation with ease of crumbling and its absence, and made the opposite of crumbly and dry [to be] solid, and it is not hidden that it is not opposite to them. His saying: (So it is either difficulty of adhesion and separation etc.) It is said: thus there would be an intermediate between them, because what is difficult in one of them and easy in the other is neither wet nor dry. For this reason the Imam said: this definition is more worthy of solidity. His saying: (Or difficulty of shaping and leaving it) This definition is objected to because it applies to solidity as it exists among the philosophers, unless the entailment of solidity for dryness is established, and that the difficulty in the solid body is due to its dryness and not due to its solidity; and how can that be proven?
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage provides a series of comments (qāla) that refine and critique the definitions of dryness. The first comment points out a problem: if dryness is defined as easy separation and difficult cohesion (as per al-Razi), then dryness would not be a tactile quality (since we feel hardness, not dryness per se), stone would not be dry (because stone is not easily separated), and fire would be wet (since fire parts easily separate but do not readily reunite). The commentator suggests that perhaps what is meant here is 'aridity' (jafāf) rather than the philosophical quality. The second comment clarifies that the Imam (al-Razi) mentioned the viscous in a different chapter. The third comment notes that in al-Mulakhkhas, the author sufficed with ease of crumbling and its opposite (solidity), but this is problematic because solidity is not the true opposite of both crumbly and dry. The fourth comment addresses the first definition (difficulty of adhesion and separation): if dryness is defined that way, there would be an intermediate state where one is difficult and the other easy, leading to something neither wet nor dry. Hence the Imam considered that the definition is more appropriate for solidity. The fifth comment critiques the second definition (difficulty of shaping): it would apply to solidity as well, unless one proves that solidity entails dryness and that the difficulty in a solid body is due to dryness, not solidity—something very hard to prove.

الكيفية
الجفاف
الملموسات
سهلة التفرق
عسيرة الاجتماع

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think dryness is simply the absence of wetness.
Correction: The passage shows that dryness as a quality involves specific causal properties (easy separation, difficult cohesion) and that its definition must be precise to avoid contradictions.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, which of the following is a consequence of defining dryness as easy separation and difficult cohesion?
A) Fire would be dry
B) Stone would be dry
C) Fire would be wet
D) Water would be dry

Key Takeaway
A faulty definition of dryness leads to absurd conclusions, highlighting the need for precise definitions in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The commentator rejected a definition because it led to absurd conclusions. Are there beliefs you hold about Allah or the unseen that, if carefully examined, lead to logical contradictions? Will you honestly re-examine them today?
?
The passage shows that reinterpreting a term (from yubūsa to jafāf) resolved the problem. In your own life, where have you clung to a wrong understanding of a concept (like worship, success, or patience) that caused inconsistencies in your actions? What correction is needed to align with truth?

Dryness and Solidity: A Misguided Equation
Critique of the definition in al-Mulakhkhas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why equating dryness to ease of crumbling and its opposite to solidity is problematic.

Core Idea
Solidity is not the true opposite of both crumbliness and dryness.

The commentator turns to a definition found in al-Mulakhkhas that equates dryness with ease of crumbling and sets its opposite as solidity. This simple equation, however, fails to capture the proper relationship between these qualities. Let's examine why.

Guided Path
1
Read the relevant passage
Read the Arabic sentences dealing with al-Mulakhkhas and the critique.
2
Understand the definition
The definition in al-Mulakhkhas sufficed with ease of crumbling (al-infirāk) as the meaning of dryness, and made solidity (al-ṣulb) the opposite of both dryness and crumbliness.
3
Identify the flaw
The commentator notes that solidity is not truly opposite to both crumbly and dry separately. Think about why.
4
Reflect on implications
This shows that definitions must be precise and not conflate different qualities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فاليبوسة حينئذ هي الكيفية إلخ ) على هذا لا تكون اليبوسة من الملموسات ولا يكون الحجر يابساً ويكون النار رطباً، لأنه وإن كانت سهلة التفرق لكنها ليست عسيرة الاجتماع أو يكون واسطة ولعل هذه اليبوسة بمعنى الجفاف. فإن الجسم المبتل إذا أثر فيه الرطوبة الغريبة يصعب تفرقه ويسهل اجتماعه عما كان قبله . وإذا جف صار الأمر بالعكس.
قوله : ( في التقسيم المنسوب إلخ) لكن ذكره الإمام في فصل بيان الهشاشة واللزوجة .
قوله: (والمذكور إلخ) يعني اكتفى في تفسيرها بسهولة الانفراك وعدمها وجعل مقابل الهش واليابس، الصلب ولا يخفى أنه ليس مقابلا لهما .
قوله : ( فهي إما عسر الالتصاق والانفصال إلخ) قيل : فعلى هذا يكون بينهما واسطة إذ ما يعسر به واحد منها، ويسهل الآخر فهو لا رطب ولا يابس ولهذا قال الإمام : هذا التعريف بالصلابة أجدر .
قوله : ( أو عسر التشكل وتركه يرد على هذا التعريف بأنه صادق على الصلابة الموجودة عند الفلاسفة اللهم إلا أن يثبت استلزام الصلابة لليبوسة وإن ذلك العسر في الجسم الصلب لأجل يبوسته لا لأجل صلابته وأنى ذلك الإثبات ؟
His saying: (So dryness then is the quality etc.) According to this, dryness is not among the tangible things, and stone would not be dry, and fire would be wet, because although it is easily divisible, it is not difficult to unite, or there would be an intermediate. Perhaps this dryness means aridity. For a moist body, when affected by extraneous wetness, becomes difficult to separate and easy to unite compared to its previous state. And when it dries, the opposite occurs. His saying: (In the division attributed etc.) But the Imam mentioned it in the chapter on explaining crumbliness and viscosity. His saying: (And it is mentioned etc.) means he sufficed in its interpretation with ease of crumbling and its absence, and made the opposite of crumbly and dry [to be] solid, and it is not hidden that it is not opposite to them. His saying: (So it is either difficulty of adhesion and separation etc.) It is said: thus there would be an intermediate between them, because what is difficult in one of them and easy in the other is neither wet nor dry. For this reason the Imam said: this definition is more worthy of solidity. His saying: (Or difficulty of shaping and leaving it) This definition is objected to because it applies to solidity as it exists among the philosophers, unless the entailment of solidity for dryness is established, and that the difficulty in the solid body is due to its dryness and not due to its solidity; and how can that be proven?
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage provides a series of comments (qāla) that refine and critique the definitions of dryness. The first comment points out a problem: if dryness is defined as easy separation and difficult cohesion (as per al-Razi), then dryness would not be a tactile quality (since we feel hardness, not dryness per se), stone would not be dry (because stone is not easily separated), and fire would be wet (since fire parts easily separate but do not readily reunite). The commentator suggests that perhaps what is meant here is 'aridity' (jafāf) rather than the philosophical quality. The second comment clarifies that the Imam (al-Razi) mentioned the viscous in a different chapter. The third comment notes that in al-Mulakhkhas, the author sufficed with ease of crumbling and its opposite (solidity), but this is problematic because solidity is not the true opposite of both crumbly and dry. The fourth comment addresses the first definition (difficulty of adhesion and separation): if dryness is defined that way, there would be an intermediate state where one is difficult and the other easy, leading to something neither wet nor dry. Hence the Imam considered that the definition is more appropriate for solidity. The fifth comment critiques the second definition (difficulty of shaping): it would apply to solidity as well, unless one proves that solidity entails dryness and that the difficulty in a solid body is due to dryness, not solidity—something very hard to prove.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that solidity and dryness are the same thing.
Correction: The passage shows they are distinct; solidity is not the opposite of dryness.

Checkpoint
What is the commentator's objection to the definition in al-Mulakhkhas?
A) It defines dryness as ease of crumbling
B) It makes solidity the opposite of both crumbly and dry
C) It ignores viscosity
D) It uses a different author

Key Takeaway
Definitions must carefully distinguish between different qualities; conflating opposites leads to error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you conflated two distinct concepts because they seemed similar, only to later realize the error? What did that cost you in understanding? Will you commit to examining definitions more carefully from now on?
?
The commentator here dares to correct a respected scholar's definition. What truth have you been afraid to speak because you respect the source? What would it take for you to speak it?
?
If your fundamental definitions of key beliefs are imprecise, what might be built on that faulty foundation? What in your own understanding of tawḥīd or ṣifāt might need re-examination today?
